    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_96272297" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96272297" />
    <a title="Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1605591-christine"><img alt="Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1252166169p2/1605591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96272297?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christine" href="/user/show/1605591-christine">Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140345353" href="/book/show/1033828.The_Devil_s_Arithmetic">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=sci-fi-time-travel">sci-fi-time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=historical-wwii">historical-wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=minority-and-women-writers">minority-and-women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1605591-christine?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96272297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10278525478297292071">This semester I am requiring my students to read <i>The True Story of Hansel and Gretel</i>, a novel takes place in Poland during World War II. The good news is that my students love the book; in fact, several of them are reading ahead.<br/><br/>	The shocking fact, the bad news, is what they don’t know. It is not just knowledge of history that they lack; it is knowledge of basic geography.<br/><br/>	God bless PowerPoint and blackboard.<br/><br/>	To be fair, my students do ask intelligent questions, yet the lack of basic knowledge i</span>
  <span id="freeText10278525478297292071" style="display:none">This semester I am requiring my students to read <i>The True Story of Hansel and Gretel</i>, a novel takes place in Poland during World War II. The good news is that my students love the book; in fact, several of them are reading ahead.<br /><br />	The shocking fact, the bad news, is what they don’t know. It is not just knowledge of history that they lack; it is knowledge of basic geography.<br /><br />	God bless PowerPoint and blackboard.<br /><br />	To be fair, my students do ask intelligent questions, yet the lack of basic knowledge is shocking. At times, I feel like I am teaching a culture and history course in addition to a reading skills course.<br /><br />	Now, I don’t think it is the students’ fault. I think the fault lies with the schools as well as parents and special interest groups. Here’s why.<br /><br />	There is a group called PABBIS (Parents against Bad Books in Schools). I disagree with them on so many different levels, but their website does have two good features. It actually quotes the material they find objectionable, and it has a complete list of banned and challenged books (as recent as 4-5 years ago).<br /><br />	<i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is on that list by the way.<br /><br />	Everything by Stephen King makes the list. Everything by Dahl, Blume, Block. There are a few <i>Black Stallion</i> books on the list as is <i>Black Beauty</i>, <i>Harry Potter</i>, <i>His Dark Materials</i>, <i>Three to Tango</i>, <i>The Diary of Anne Frank</i>, <i>The Lorax</i>, and Shakespeare.<br /><br />	In other words, pretty much anything good.<br /><br />	Lately, history and science text books have been subjected to strange changes (i.e. as in Texas) and warning labels (usually about evolution).<br /><br />	In terms banning and challenging books, I might under the idea if the book was being assigned at an inappropriate grade level, say <i>Beloved</i> to first graders. Why an exceptional first grader might not have a problem with such a book, there are several things wrong, in general, about such assignment, least of which is the subject matter. Yet, I have never heard of such an assignment. Age appropriateness isn’t really the issue. It seems to rest on what might offend any group, anywhere.<br /><br />	Take, for instance, <i>The Diary of Anne Frank</i>. This book has been banned, challenged, and rejected by parents and text book committees because of the sex and tragedy of the story.<br /><br />	<i>Raisin in the Sun</i> has been banned and challenged because it’s pornographic. (Can someone please, please show me where?).<br /><br />	I can certainly see why <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is banned. It is the mention of a wedding.<br /><br />	Blatantly heterosexual.<br /><br />	Okay, it’s most likely due to the violence (it’s about the Holocaust) and the ending. <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> was dissed by a teacher who said it was a blatant endorsement of heterosexual love (so I guess teen suicide is okay).<br /><br />	BUT, Yolen’s magnificent book is exactly the type of book that should be used in schools.<br /><br />	Let’s face it, the wrong text book and/or the wrong teacher can make history very boring, and sometimes people just don’t like learning about history for a variety of reasons. This is the reason why good historical fiction should be used in schools. A good historical novel can get a reader interested in a period, in an event, in a person. This true, to a degree, of such less accurate work than <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i>. <i>300</i> caused some people to become interested in the Spartans, <i>Titanic</i> in the actual ship. In terms of books, vampire novels and historical fiction, such as <i>The Other Boleyn Girl</i> also cause readers to become interested in the actual events or myths that the novel is based on. Curiosity and a desire to learn are fueled by a variety of things; interest is one of them.<br /><br />	<i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is precisely the type of book that for young readers can help history seem more real and, perhaps, get a reader interested in history. Yolen does not talk down to her readers, her main character is sympathetic, an older sibling, and because Yolen doesn’t pull her punches, it is a real history, not a feel good history. This makes the story far more compelling and interesting. The story is told actively and quickly. Words are not wasted. The reader learns but is not lectured to and screamed at.<br /><br />	Because of this reality and vividness, parents object to the story, and this raises the question of whether teachers and parents should sugarcoat history. It’s true that are plenty of novels and movies that present history in a more flattering light (look at the perfect teeth, the clean bodies, the small pox vaccination scars). A reader can quite easily find it in any average romance novel. Such writing does serve a sense of purpose, escapism, and there is nothing wrong with that.<br /><br />	But that is not education.<br /><br />	Is this what parents want a school to be?<br /><br />	I hope not. I suppose it is easy for me to comment on <br />appropriateness and the evil of banning because I don’t have a child, and I was raised in a house where you were encouraged to read what you wanted. I see, however, the effects of banning and challenging which are a total lack of knowledge or, worse, a lack of interest about a subject because the subject has been taught in such a sterile environment, so devoid of any color or shading, where everything is the color and taste of sugar; where everyone in the world has always treated everyone with respect and love, where women always had the right to vote, and slavery only existed in North and South America, and was totally destroyed by the American Civil War.<br /><br />	Good literature, of which <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is a prime example forces its readers to examine their own knowledge and lack of knowledge, forces readers to think about their own responses, notions, and stereotypes. Good literature teaches or enlightens the reader even if the reader already knows. It is a bond between writer, book, and reader that is no less real than those ties of family and society that provide the excuse for book banning.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10278525478297292071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77553243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15800455771124382165">I wish I could say I liked this book. I thought I would. I know it's critically acclaimed and a well-known story. But it left me with a bad taste in my mouth.<br/><br/>The book is meant to educate young people about the Holocaust, but it had a lot of historical inaccuracies. The idyllic shtetl world at the beginning of Chaya's story would have been long gone by 1942 -- by that time, all the Jews left alive in Poland were in ghettos, in concentration camps or in hiding. Lublin, the place Chaya supposedly c</span>
  <span id="freeText15800455771124382165" style="display:none">I wish I could say I liked this book. I thought I would. I know it's critically acclaimed and a well-known story. But it left me with a bad taste in my mouth.<br /><br />The book is meant to educate young people about the Holocaust, but it had a lot of historical inaccuracies. The idyllic shtetl world at the beginning of Chaya's story would have been long gone by 1942 -- by that time, all the Jews left alive in Poland were in ghettos, in concentration camps or in hiding. Lublin, the place Chaya supposedly came from, was ghettoized and in early 1942, most of its Jewish population was deported to Belzec and killed.<br /><br />The dialogue was overly didactic (a common flaw in historical novels, especially those for children) and too much was told rather than shown. Further, the camp confused me. Yolen says in the end that she created an amalgam fictional camp out of various aspects of real camps, but she used the trademark Auschwitz sign: "Arbeit Macht Frei." I was confused throughout the book: This is Auschwitz? But where are the selections, the band, Mengele? Did she do any research at all, I wondered. Yolen should have revealed her use of a made-up camp at the beginning, and she shouldn't have used the Auschwitz sign.<br /><br />If you want to look for some better books on the Holocaust for children, try any of Uri Orlev's, or Jerry Spinelli's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/69392.Milkweed__Readers_Circle_" title="Milkweed (Readers Circle) by Jerry Spinelli" rel="nofollow noopener">Milkweed</a>, or Livia Bitton-Jackson's memoir <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48834.I_Have_Lived_A_Thousand_Years_Growing_Up_In_The_Holocaust" title="I Have Lived A Thousand Years Growing Up In The Holocaust by Livia Bitton-Jackson" rel="nofollow noopener">I Have Lived a Thousand Years</a>, or...well, quite a few books are better than this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15800455771124382165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3534148174" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3534148174" />
    <a title="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90347401-elsa-rajan-pradhananga"><img alt="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595732933p2/90347401.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3534148174?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elsa Rajan Pradhananga" href="/user/show/90347401-elsa-rajan-pradhananga">Elsa Rajan Pradhananga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25294421-the-devil-s-arithmetic">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3534148174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3886855260311898212">I was 14 when I read my first holocaust book – Leon Uris’ Mila 18 and was in much distress for very long because of the Nazi savagery and Jewish despondency I was exposed to in the book. Although I have since been drawn to literature from that period, I feel that the gory in <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is too harsh for anyone under 12. The heart rending portrayal of children in <i> Mapping the Bones, The Boy in the Striped Pyjamas, Anne Frank’s Diary, The Book Thief, The girl in the red coat in Schindle</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3886855260311898212" style="display:none">I was 14 when I read my first holocaust book – Leon Uris’ Mila 18 and was in much distress for very long because of the Nazi savagery and Jewish despondency I was exposed to in the book. Although I have since been drawn to literature from that period, I feel that the gory in <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is too harsh for anyone under 12. The heart rending portrayal of children in <i> Mapping the Bones, The Boy in the Striped Pyjamas, Anne Frank’s Diary, The Book Thief, The girl in the red coat in Schindler’s List…</i> make sure that the atrocities that children were subjected to during the holocaust are never forgotten.<br /><br />In <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic </i> when during the Passover, Hanna from 1990s’ New York opens the door of her grandparents’ apartment to look for Prophet Elijah, she finds herself in Poland of 1941 as Chaya. The very next day, she is sent off to a concentration camp where surrounded by cruelty she grows up too quickly and too seriously. Hope kept her alive as she stuck to senseless rules to increase her chance at survival. Chaya’s story ends abruptly with a pang and by the time she is transported back to the apartment in New York, she has matured into a person who understood the significance of remembering. <br /><br />The plot device used in this historical fiction puts young readers at ease because they know that despite the hardships Chaya faces, she will become Hannah by the end of the book. A really moving read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3886855260311898212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6269549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6269549" />
    <a title="Lisa Vegan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83445-lisa-vegan"><img alt="Lisa Vegan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243394822p2/83445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6269549?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa Vegan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa Vegan" href="/user/show/83445-lisa-vegan">Lisa Vegan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670810277" href="/book/show/1033829.The_Devil_s_Arithmetic">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everybody at least 12 and up</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=z2008">z2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=readbooks-female-author-or-illust">readbooks-female-author-or-illust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=goodreads-author">goodreads-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6269549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17169002497704492564">This is a marvelous book for young adults, although I wouldn’t recommend it as their first introduction to the holocaust because it portrays the atrocities committed in a starkly realistic way. And, unlike some young adult books that I enjoyed as young as nine or ten years old, I wouldn’t give this to kids until they were at least 12.<br/><br/>It is a wonderful story and, because the main character, an American Jewish girl who’s 12 years old, is from the present time (even though the book was written twen</span>
  <span id="freeText17169002497704492564" style="display:none">This is a marvelous book for young adults, although I wouldn’t recommend it as their first introduction to the holocaust because it portrays the atrocities committed in a starkly realistic way. And, unlike some young adult books that I enjoyed as young as nine or ten years old, I wouldn’t give this to kids until they were at least 12.<br /><br />It is a wonderful story and, because the main character, an American Jewish girl who’s 12 years old, is from the present time (even though the book was written twenty years ago, it still has a sufficiently modern feel) readers can perhaps empathize with her and the experiences she has when she goes back in time to Poland during the Nazi holocaust. I thought Jane Yolen had a truly inspired way to tell this story. Even readers who do not normally enjoy history or historical fiction will probably appreciate this book because the time travel nature of the story allows a contemporary young person to truly experience events of the past. (I’ve always loved history and historical fiction, but I know that some young people are not so interested.)<br /><br />The characters are likeable and believable and the ending is smart and heartwarming. All through the book there are hints about how the story will evolve and in a way it was predictable, but the details of the ending caught me by surprise, even though I was looking for the gist of what did happen.<br /><br />It’s a terrific family story and it could make for intimate discussions between young adults and their adult family members. I’m also strongly in favor of it being taught in schools as part of the curricula for holocaust studies and history lessons.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17169002497704492564" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_28679406" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28679406" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28679406?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140345353" href="/book/show/1033828.The_Devil_s_Arithmetic">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone -- but especially teenagers that think they are the center of the universe! ;)</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Becky by: </span>
      Thrift store find
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=multi-dimensional">multi-dimensional</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2008">2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=ww2">ww2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28679406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12351518346371150862">I wasn't really sure what to make of this book when I first saw it, but after having read it, I would say that I am glad that I did. <br/><br/>This is one of those books that really makes you look at things from a different perspective. I can relate to Hannah, because I remember being 13 and having little patience with traditions and customs, and just wanting to hang out with my friends. <br/><br/>But given the experience Hannah had, she was able to see things in a new way, and was granted a gift, even though it wa</span>
  <span id="freeText12351518346371150862" style="display:none">I wasn't really sure what to make of this book when I first saw it, but after having read it, I would say that I am glad that I did. <br /><br />This is one of those books that really makes you look at things from a different perspective. I can relate to Hannah, because I remember being 13 and having little patience with traditions and customs, and just wanting to hang out with my friends. <br /><br />But given the experience Hannah had, she was able to see things in a new way, and was granted a gift, even though it was at a great cost, to be able to know and really understand her family's past and how they became who they are. And because of this, she gains a newfound respect and admiration for them, and her own life, that she might not have otherwise known. <br /><br />This is the lesson that this book taught me. Yes, it was about the Holocaust and the epic tragedy that occurred, but I think it was more about understanding and respecting where you come from, and not letting trivial everyday teenage life get in the way of honoring your past.<br /><br />***SPOILERS BELOW***<br /><br />Ultimately, I gave this one 4 stars only because the book never really explained who/where <i>Chaya</i> was really. <br />With these types of books, where someone goes back in time into the body of another person, I always wonder where the person who is inhabited goes when the person who is inhabiting them is there. <br /><br />Did <i>Chaya</i> die when she was ill, allowing Hannah to come back in order make her a hero to her Aunt? Or did <i>Chaya</i> sort of get shunted off to the side when Hannah took over, which means that <i>Chaya</i> had no choice in the sacrifice she made?<br /><br />I hope the latter is not the case, although near the end it is mentioned that Hannah has 3 sets of memories -- of being in Lublin, of being with Gitl and Schmuel, and of her American family. It seems to me that Hannah should only have had 2 sets of memories if <i>Chaya</i> was not in there somewhere. <br /><br />The last possibility is that <i>Chaya</i> was Hannah in a past life, whose life Hannah had a vision of (through <i>Chaya's</i> eyes, perhaps?) at just the right moment to attain the perspective she needed... Of the three, this is the most appealing to me, although some aspects of the story don't fit perfectly with this theory. <br /><br />Overall, I am very glad that I read this book, and would highly recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12351518346371150862" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42181003" />
    <a title="K8" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1876250-k8"><img alt="K8" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316142535p2/1876250.jpg" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42181003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7040687092743263741">Twelve year old Hannah is sick of spending Passover 'remembering' the past with her relatives. During the Passover Seder, she is transported to 1942 Poland, where she becomes Chaya (her Hebrew name), the girl she was named for. In this time, she is eventually sent to a concentration camp, where the bulk of the story takes place. Throughout the book, she struggles with memory - which memories are real (the future or the now), remembering anything b/c of the trauma of the camp, futilely trying to </span>
  <span id="freeText7040687092743263741" style="display:none">Twelve year old Hannah is sick of spending Passover 'remembering' the past with her relatives. During the Passover Seder, she is transported to 1942 Poland, where she becomes Chaya (her Hebrew name), the girl she was named for. In this time, she is eventually sent to a concentration camp, where the bulk of the story takes place. Throughout the book, she struggles with memory - which memories are real (the future or the now), remembering anything b/c of the trauma of the camp, futilely trying to use her future-memory to warn those around her, etc.<br /><br />The story is chilling. And it is beautiful and sad. And it is an amazing combination of historical fiction and s/f.<br /><br />Some of my favorite quotations:<br /><br />"Passover isn't about eating, Hannah," her mother began at last, sighing and pushing her fingers through her silver-streaked hair. "You could have fooled me," Hannah muttered. (4)<br /><br />But as the scissors snip-snapped through her hair and the razor shaved the rest, she realized with a sudden awful panic that she could no longer recall anything from the past. I cannot remember, she whispered to herself. I cannot remember. She's been shorn of memory as brutally as she'd been shorn of her hair, without permission, without reason...Gone, all gone, she thought again wildly, no longer even sure what was gone, what she was mourning. (94)<br /><br />"We all have such stories. It is a brutal arithmetic. But I - I am alive. You are alive. As long as we breathe, we can see and hear. As long as we can remember, all those gone before are alive inside us." (113)<br /><br />The days' routines were as before, the only change being the constant redness of the sky as trainloads of nameless zugangi were shipped along the rails of death. Still the camp seemed curiously lightened because of it, as if everyone knew that as long as others were processed, they would not be. A simple bit of mathematics, like subtraction, where on taken away at the top line becomes one added on to the bottom. The Devil's arithmetic." (146)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7040687092743263741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_37737106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37737106" />
    <a title="Lindsey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1712996-lindsey"><img alt="Lindsey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1227415664p2/1712996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37737106?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsey" href="/user/show/1712996-lindsey">Lindsey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lindsey by: </span>
      My children's lit professor

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37737106" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8103055731067192561">Anyone and everyone should read this book! It's a very fast read because it was written for children but it tells a beautiful story and has a great twist in the end. The Devil's Arithmetic is about a young Jewish girl who doesn't quite understand her family's past. She finds Jewish holidays and celebrations to be boring and is unappreciative of the hardships Jews have faced. She is mysteriously transported to the past and ends up in a concentration camp. Here she suffers the hardships first hand</span>
  <span id="freeText8103055731067192561" style="display:none">Anyone and everyone should read this book! It's a very fast read because it was written for children but it tells a beautiful story and has a great twist in the end. The Devil's Arithmetic is about a young Jewish girl who doesn't quite understand her family's past. She finds Jewish holidays and celebrations to be boring and is unappreciative of the hardships Jews have faced. She is mysteriously transported to the past and ends up in a concentration camp. Here she suffers the hardships first hand and begins to understand and appreciate her family's history. Heads up, it's a tear-jerker!</span>

<div id="review_3262974729" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3262974729" />
    <a title="Abigail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail"><img alt="Abigail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539121384p2/87835420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3262974729?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail">Abigail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670810277" href="/book/show/1033829.The_Devil_s_Arithmetic">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers Looking for Middle-Grade Fiction About the Holocaust</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fantasy">childrens-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=it-broke-my-heart">it-broke-my-heart</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=jewish-children">jewish-children</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=time-travel">time-travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3262974729" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer672713269743886718">For me, picking up a book about the Holocaust is a bit like plunging into an icy-cold lake, on a warm summer day. Not because the experience is refreshing - far from it! - but because there is this sense, while standing on the edge, poised to take that fateful step, of drawing back. An instinctive recoiling from what I know will be a sudden and shocking submersion in a different world - one that I'm never entirely prepared to enter, that I fear will swallow me whole, as I sink like a stone, down</span>
  <span id="freeText672713269743886718" style="display:none">For me, picking up a book about the Holocaust is a bit like plunging into an icy-cold lake, on a warm summer day. Not because the experience is refreshing - far from it! - but because there is this sense, while standing on the edge, poised to take that fateful step, of drawing back. An instinctive recoiling from what I know will be a sudden and shocking submersion in a different world - one that I'm never entirely prepared to enter, that I fear will swallow me whole, as I sink like a stone, down and down into the cold, dark depths. <br /><br />Since the day I stumbled across my first Holocaust memoir - Sara Zyskind's <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3098271.Stolen_Years" title="Stolen Years by Sara Zyskind" rel="nofollow noopener">Stolen Years</a>
</u>, which chronicles the author's time in the Lodz Ghetto, and then the Auschwitz-Birkenau death-camp - the year I was eleven, I have had an abiding interest in this terrible episode of history, and a desire to understand what made it, and other genocides, possible. I have, over the years, read numerous survivor testimonies, and devoted much time to considering the nature of human evil, the ubiquity of human suffering, and the historical, cultural, and psychological factors that allow them to flourish. I believe in remembering, in bearing witness, and - to the best of my limited ability - in seeking to oppose and change those factors which facilitate such atrocities.<br /><br />But no one (amongst the "sane," anyway) can remember all the time. I don't spend every waking hour contemplating these issues, and I don't desire to. Which isn't to say I "forget" them, per se, just that the intimacy of my knowledge, of my remembering, varies greatly. I always "remember" the Holocaust. But the remembering involved in sociological analysis, and the remembering that comes of witnessing - even if only through the printed word - a vulnerable young child, violently separated from his only kin, tossed about in a maelstrom born of adult depravity, depart this world through the doors of the gas chamber, and the smokestacks of those infernal human-powered ovens, are two very different things. To read a book about the Holocaust, be it fact or fiction, is to embrace that second kind of remembering, to become acquainted, once again, with madness.<br /><br /><br />And that seems like an entirely appropriate jumping-off point to me, because this book, this children's novel, is about nothing so much as memory, and our (very natural) reluctance to embrace it. It is the story of a contemporary Jewish American girl who, reluctantly attending her family's Passover Seder, opens the door for the Prophet Elijah, and finds herself in 1940s Poland. Hannah Stern of New Rochelle (five minutes from where I myself live) is now Chaya Abramowicz of Lublin, and a suburban girl who has always lived a life of privilege and plenty is about to discover a world of unimaginable loss and privation. For the small Jewish shtetl in which she finds herself is about to be liquidated - transported to one of the Nazi death camps...<br /><br /><u>The Devil's Arithmetic</u> is a powerful argument for the importance - the necessity - of remembering, but it is also a meditation on the difficulty of convincing others of the truth, and the limitations of knowledge itself, when confronting the full power of evil unleashed. Hannah/Chaya knows what is coming - she knows what those Nazis and their trucks in front of the village shul mean, she knows where the cattle cars are headed. But although she attempts to warn the others, tries desperately to convince them to flee, no one will believe her. She is, after all, just a child - a child known to say odd things, because of a recent illness - and <i>what</i> she is saying is so unimaginably terrifying to her listeners, that she is silenced - hushed by her "Aunt" Gitl.<br /><br />Eventually, she is silenced by the loss of memory itself. Horrified, the first night in camp, by the showers toward which she and the other women are being herded, convinced that they are really the gas chambers about which she had learned, in her other life, Hannah/Chaya once again attempts to warn the others. But finally, perceiving that they <i>cannot</i> hear her words, that she is only robbing them of their last protection, robbing them of hope, she desists, only to find this silence reinforced by a horrifying inability to recall who she really is, and what lies ahead:<br /><br /><i>When the man came to Hannah, she bit her lip so as not to cry and kept her eyes closed the entire time. She concentrated on what was to happen next - after the showers and the hair-cutting, remembering from the lessons in Holocaust history in school. But as the scissors snip-snapped through her hair and the razor shaved the rest, she realized with a sudden awful panic that she could no longer recall anything from the past. "I cannot remember," she whispered to herself. "I cannot remember." She'd been shorn of memory as brutally as she'd been shorn of her hair, without permission, without reason. Opening her eyes, she stared at the floor. Clots of wet hair lay all about: dark hair, light hair, short hair, long hair, and two pale braids. "Gone, all gone," she thought again wildly, no longer even sure what was gone, what she was mourning."</i><br /><br />Hannah, the girl who didn't want to remember the past, has now become Chaya, the girl who cannot remember the future - a future the Nazis are intent on destroying.<br /><br /><br />Jane Yolen has created a powerful story in <u>The Devil's Arithmetic</u>, one that will draw young readers in, allowing them - through the plot device of a modern child traveling back through time - to experience the terror of the Holocaust in a uniquely intimate way. That it is necessary for them do so - to enter into this strange and horrifying world of the past, and become one with the victims - is borne out, not just by the maxim that "those who forget the past are doomed to repeat it," but by the reality that we are all vulnerable, not just to those forces which might make us victims, but to those which might make us monsters. As Fayge says, at one point, quoting from a story about the Ba'al Shem Tov: <i>"The enemy will always be with you. He will be in the shadow of your dreams and in your living flesh, for he is the other part of yourself."</i><br /><br />This "other part" always needs combating. What better way to do this, than to witness the terrible cruelty and suffering that result, when it is unleashed? So... take a deep breath, grab hold of your courage, fix your eyes on the truth, and plunge into that dark water. It has to be done. It <i>always</i> has to be done. And it always has to be done <i>again</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="672713269743886718" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_74187263" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74187263" />
    <a title="Janette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1292612-janette"><img alt="Janette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442459284p2/1292612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74187263?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janette" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janette" href="/user/show/1292612-janette">Janette</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74187263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6327396107577329572">I usually read to avoid hearing about depressing subjects but I went ahead and read this one even though it was about a Jewish girl living during WW2.<br/><br/>It was a good book, and I got choked up in the end. Then I couldn't get to sleep at night because I was too busy pondering how civilized societies are capable of butchering millions of people. It seems so impossible, and yet it's happened more than once in history. <br/><br/>It makes you look at your friends and neighbors and wonder what sort of hearts of da</span>
  <span id="freeText6327396107577329572" style="display:none">I usually read to avoid hearing about depressing subjects but I went ahead and read this one even though it was about a Jewish girl living during WW2.<br /><br />It was a good book, and I got choked up in the end. Then I couldn't get to sleep at night because I was too busy pondering how civilized societies are capable of butchering millions of people. It seems so impossible, and yet it's happened more than once in history. <br /><br />It makes you look at your friends and neighbors and wonder what sort of hearts of darkness might be there.<br /><br />And it makes you wonder what might happen again in the future. What would I do if the government came for me or for my neighbors?<br /><br />I know it's not the purpose of the book--but it did make me want to go out and buy a gun for the first time in my life. If the Jews had been armed, it would have been a different story.<br /><br />Anyway, this was a good book and it made you think--although I go back and forth as to whether it should really be in the juvenile section. I'm not sure it's a book I'd choose for an eight year-old. I mean, if I had trouble sleeping after reading this book, it might be too much for a young child to handle. Parents should probably read it before checking it out for their children.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6327396107577329572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_40036849" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/40036849" />
    <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie"><img alt="Lars Guthrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199816668p2/757602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/40036849?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars Guthrie" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie">Lars Guthrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer40036849" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10303632382811892823">Yolen employs a "Magic Tree House" trope to move her main character, Hannah, a bored American thirteen-year-old at her family's Seder dinner, through time, space and language, and it comes off as hokey. Once Hannah becomes Chaya, an orphan living in a Polish village in 1942, though, this tale grabs onto the reader and doesn't let go. Hannah opens the door of her family's apartment to welcome the prophet Elijah and is soon crammed into a crowded cattle car with other Jews on a train destined for </span>
  <span id="freeText10303632382811892823" style="display:none">Yolen employs a "Magic Tree House" trope to move her main character, Hannah, a bored American thirteen-year-old at her family's Seder dinner, through time, space and language, and it comes off as hokey. Once Hannah becomes Chaya, an orphan living in a Polish village in 1942, though, this tale grabs onto the reader and doesn't let go. Hannah opens the door of her family's apartment to welcome the prophet Elijah and is soon crammed into a crowded cattle car with other Jews on a train destined for the "final solution." Yolen makes such a narrative more than a recounting of horror and the inhumanity of humanity with strong characters and a strong story. By the time Hannah re-enters the present and pleasant Passover gathering with heightened respect and empathy for her Holocaust-surviving grandfather and aunt, I was willing to forgive the contrived device. After all, it's one that works for kids in any number of series. And in the end, it did so here. The novel prompts young readers swept up in Yolen's carefully researched tale to make the effort to remember what must never be forgotten.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10303632382811892823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3010626958438856770">Five stars for the plot device used to tell this story. This is aimed at very young readers and so the grisly subject matter must be presented in a way that allows them to read it without ruining their sleep. Having the main character, a modern day, young Jewish girl, living in New Rochelle, transported back in time does this splendidly.<br/><br/>My three star review is because I didn't feel the story was written very well after that initial stroke of genius. I must admit that part of this could be that I</span>
  <span id="freeText3010626958438856770" style="display:none">Five stars for the plot device used to tell this story. This is aimed at very young readers and so the grisly subject matter must be presented in a way that allows them to read it without ruining their sleep. Having the main character, a modern day, young Jewish girl, living in New Rochelle, transported back in time does this splendidly.<br /><br />My three star review is because I didn't feel the story was written very well after that initial stroke of genius. I must admit that part of this could be that I was expecting a book aimed at teens and this was clearly meant for even younger readers, but I still struggled through after the set up.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer943286213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12112069748269345606">So, during school (L.A) we're learning about World War II and the Holocaust, I've always wanted to learn more about both topics. My teacher suggested this book to me, and I'm happy that she did. I've never, so far, read a book like this in my life. First of all, this was the first Holocaust book I've read. I just love how this book starts and ends, sort of like a circular ending. Most books wont make me shed tears but this one did. I think it was amazing that Hannah the main character, got to go</span>
  <span id="freeText12112069748269345606" style="display:none">So, during school (L.A) we're learning about World War II and the Holocaust, I've always wanted to learn more about both topics. My teacher suggested this book to me, and I'm happy that she did. I've never, so far, read a book like this in my life. First of all, this was the first Holocaust book I've read. I just love how this book starts and ends, sort of like a circular ending. Most books wont make me shed tears but this one did. I think it was amazing that Hannah the main character, got to go back in time to 1942! It was nice to see things from her perspective. This kept me at the edge of my seat, it was a little scary not knowing what was going to happen next. I really enjoyed this book, and I recommend it to anyone who is looking for a good Holocaust book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12112069748269345606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2772351226&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2772351226" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >

I wish it could have told what happened to Hannah/Chaya and her friend Ester and Shifre after they were "chosen" although it might have been s</span>
  <span id="freeText319221194658213403" style="display:none">This book is about a girl named chaya and Hannah (they are the same person) going back in time to the Nazi camps and learning about how she should always remember the past and always remember everything. i liked this book a lot because i got to learn more about what evil people the Nazis were and what the Jewish Greek and others were forced to go through. <div id="review_1104389761" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1104389761" />
    <a title="Luthien" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3890428-luthien"><img alt="Luthien" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468848475p2/3890428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1104389761?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luthien" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luthien" href="/user/show/3890428-luthien">Luthien</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3890428-luthien?shelf=ya-done-right">ya-done-right</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3890428-luthien?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3890428-luthien?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1104389761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17706944869016302745">Giving this book a star rating and a review feels…strangely inappropriate. No piece of literature is above critique, of course, but after I finished this one, I felt like I had the wind knocked out of me a little. Though it wasn’t flawless, but deconstructing it for a full-fledged review didn’t feel like a constructive exercise. . There are undoubtedly small inaccuracies which older readers, in particular, might find frustrating, but the spirit of the book matters more to me given the themes and</span>
  <span id="freeText17706944869016302745" style="display:none">Giving this book a star rating and a review feels…strangely inappropriate. No piece of literature is above critique, of course, but after I finished this one, I felt like I had the wind knocked out of me a little. Though it wasn’t flawless, but deconstructing it for a full-fledged review didn’t feel like a constructive exercise. . There are undoubtedly small inaccuracies which older readers, in particular, might find frustrating, but the spirit of the book matters more to me given the themes and audience of the story.<br /><br />No matter how many times you read or hear about the Holocaust, the full horror of it remains utterly debilitating.<br /><br />I’m giving it four stars instead of five for a few reasons. As a literary work, it is on par with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7824322.Between_Shades_of_Gray" title="Between Shades of Gray by Ruta Sepetys" rel="nofollow noopener">Between Shades of Gray</a>, which I also gave four stars—this one is written for a slightly younger audience, though it’s no less emotionally riveting. I also feel it may be a little <i>too</i> intense for its intended audience (readers between twelve and fourteen or so). Jane Yolen does not pull punches. Everything short of dying women and children clawing at the walls of the gas chamber is in her. My library has it filed under “Older Teens” in the YA section.<br /><br />The novel is, however, still relatively well-written from an adult perspective. Yolen keeps her prose simple and to-the-point. The parts in the book that take place in the present dragged a little, and modern Hannah’s voice annoyed me (on purpose, I’m sure). I’m not Jewish, but I have attended three or four Passover Seders—if I hadn’t, I’m sure the beginning would have totally confused me, but the dark twist on the “Dorothy goes to Oz” trope during the Seder was clever and well-done.<br /><br />By keeping the cast of characters to a minimum, Yolen also maximizes each character’s importance to Hannah, to the reader, and to the story as a whole. Most of them aren’t fully fleshed out (the novel is more of a novella at just 170 pages), but they’re real enough to tug at the reader’s heartstrings. The Holocaust is often dealt with numerically—even by Hannah herself as she frantically tries to warn her skeptical friends that the Nazis will murder six million Jews. Yolen does a good job of scaling the immediate tragedy down to a handful of that number, thereby humanizing and individualizing the victims.<br /><br />The camp narrative, which takes up about half of the novel, was just fictionalized enough—and given the staggering number of victims and Yolen’s own research, something like it very well <i>could</i> have happened even if this particular narrative is fictional. As I said, she doesn’t shy away from the fear and the trauma, though fortunately she keeps the graphic content (blood, waste, etc.) to a bare minimum. It’s no less horrific for being rather simple. Elie Wiesel’s very real memories in <i>Night</i> are recounted in a similar style.<br /><br />Though the time-travel setup is a bit hokey, I did like the way Yolen handled Hannah’s fading memories and foreknowledge. It fit nicely with the theme of hope, however small, and the need to keep it alive from day to day in order to survive. Would it be better to know the full horror that awaited you—the truth about these “factories of death”—Yolen asks through Hannah, or to be ignorant? <br /><br />My introduction to the Holocaust was the decidedly milder <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47281.Number_the_Stars" title="Number the Stars by Lois Lowry" rel="nofollow noopener">Number the Stars</a> in elementary school, and my first serious contact with it came when I was fourteen during a visit to the Holocaust Museum in D.C.. I’m not sure there’s an age when a child is “ready” to be exposed to that sort of horror, but <i>The Devil’s Arithmetic</i> is not a book I would give to anyone under twelve or thirteen.<br /><br />Effective, touching, haunting, and sometimes poetic, this short novel is worth a few hours of your time even if you fall outside its target audience.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17706944869016302745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_307581932" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307581932" />
    <a title="Alex  Baugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4449236-alex-baugh"><img alt="Alex  Baugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439208555p2/4449236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307581932?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex  Baugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex  Baugh" href="/user/show/4449236-alex-baugh">Alex  Baugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140345353" href="/book/show/1033828.The_Devil_s_Arithmetic">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4449236-alex-baugh?shelf=world-war-2">world-war-2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307581932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9819390388866538263">Since tonight is the first night of Passover, I thought I would review a book that is appropriate to the season. I chose The Devil's Arithmetic because, like the Passover story, it is also about the importance of remembering who you are and where you came from. <br/><br/>Hannah Stern, 12 but almost 13, is a happy girl living in New Rochelle with her parents and little brother, except that she doesn't want to go to her family's Passover Seder. Bored and apathetic, Hannah is tired of hearing her grandfather</span>
  <span id="freeText9819390388866538263" style="display:none">Since tonight is the first night of Passover, I thought I would review a book that is appropriate to the season. I chose The Devil's Arithmetic because, like the Passover story, it is also about the importance of remembering who you are and where you came from. <br /><br />Hannah Stern, 12 but almost 13, is a happy girl living in New Rochelle with her parents and little brother, except that she doesn't want to go to her family's Passover Seder. Bored and apathetic, Hannah is tired of hearing her grandfather's stories about the past, including those about the concentration camp where he spent part of his youth. But this year's Passover holds a big surprise for Hannah.<br /><br />When she goes to open the door to let Elijah in, Hannah suddenly finds herself transported back in time to a Polish shetl in 1942, where her name is Chaya Abramowitz and she is living with her Aunt Gitl and Uncle Schmuel. Hannah learns that Chaya parents have recently died and she has been very sick. The next day, Chaya goes with her family to another shetl where Uncle Schmuel is to be married. But before that can happen, the entire residents of both shetls are rounded up by Nazi soldiers and sent on a days long train ride in a cattle car to a concentration/extermination camp. <br /><br />Before she knows it, the men and women are separated, their clothes are taken away, their hair is shaved off and a number is tattooed on their left arms. Chaya and Aunt Gitl are assigned to a barracks and give jobs to do.<br /><br />Working in the kitchen, Chaya meets Rivka, a wise ten year old who has survived life in the camp for a year and knows how to do things to avoid being "chosen" by the camp commandant for "processing." So far, Chaya, Rivka and their other friends have been lucky, but can their luck hold out? And will Chaya ever become Hannah again and return to her present day family?<br /><br />The Devil's Arithmetic is a wonderfully well-written middle grade novel. The randomness of life for Jews under the Nazis is captured so well, as it the horror their methods, yet not to the point where it is so graphic it would turn kids away from the book or from learning about the Holocaust, but it does help understand at least the how of what happened.<br /><br />And yes, Hannah does remember what she had learned about the Holocaust in school and home, but little by little she finds her memory fading. Even her present day family begins to recede from her memory. And she does try to warn everyone about what happened to Jews in the Holocaust, but they find it so outlandish that they can't comprehend what she says. And who could blame them? <br /><br />While Hannah's experiences in the concentration camp point to the importance of remembering, they also demonstrates the perils of forgetting, a good lesson for us all to think about during this holiday season. <br /><br />This book is recommended for readers age 10 and up<br />This book was purchased for my personal library<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9819390388866538263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41242370" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Loralee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1817661-loralee"><img alt="Loralee" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41242370?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Loralee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Loralee" href="/user/show/1817661-loralee">Loralee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1817661-loralee?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1817661-loralee?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer41242370&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer41242370" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11084384788806389991">This touching book uses time travel to help one young girl understand her Jewish heritage. One of the main themes is not to forget the past but learn from it. These memories include remembering the Holocaust.<br/><br/>The book opens with Hannah being reminded why she celebrates Passover. "Passover isn't about eating, Hannah....It's about remembering." (p.4) Once Hannah is transported back into time and begins living the life of Chaya she reflects on the things she's learned at family dinners and in classr</span>
  <span id="freeText11084384788806389991" style="display:none">This touching book uses time travel to help one young girl understand her Jewish heritage. One of the main themes is not to forget the past but learn from it. These memories include remembering the Holocaust.<br /><br />The book opens with Hannah being reminded why she celebrates Passover. "Passover isn't about eating, Hannah....It's about remembering." (p.4) Once Hannah is transported back into time and begins living the life of Chaya she reflects on the things she's learned at family dinners and in classrooms. "She kept remembering more and more, bits and pieces of her classroom discussions about the Holocaust. About the death camps and the crematoria. About the brutal Nazis and the six million dead Jews...A strange awful taste rose in her mouth, more bitter even than the Seder's bitter herbs. And they were for remembering." (p. 72) <br /><br />When she reaches the concentration camp, she's told she must remember her new identity. "'You are Chaya no longer, child. Now you are J197241. Remember it.' 'I can't remember anything,' Hannah said, puzzled. "This you must remember, for if you forget it, life is gone indeed.'" (p. 100) <br /><br />Once Hannah returned to present day life, she discovered that her aunt was her old friend in the concentration camp. "Aunt Eve closed her eyes for a moment, as if thinking or remembering. Then she whispered back, 'His name was Wolfe. Wolfe! And the irony of it was that he was as gentle as a lamb. He changed his name when we came to America. We all changed our names. To forget. Remembering was too painful. But to forget was impossible.'" (p. 163)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11084384788806389991" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11629256539178715002"> This was really sad and scary. But so REALISTIC!! It's terrifying to think that it acually would happen! (Not the time traveling, the concentration camp.)<br/><br/> It is about a Jewish girl, Hannah, who is at a passover dinner, when she goes through a door and finds herself in 1942.   <span id="freeText11629256539178715002" style="display:none"> This was really sad and scary. But so REALISTIC!! It's terrifying to think that it acually would happen! (Not the time traveling, the concentration camp.)<br /><br /> It is about a Jewish girl, Hannah, who is at a passover dinner, when she goes through a door and finds herself in 1942. Everyone knows who she is except herself. They think she is an orphan girl called Chaya, which is what her name is in some language. She makes five friends; Rachel, Shirfre, Ester, Yente, and Rivka. Rachel and Yente die on the way to the concentration camp, so they don't have a big part in the book.<br /> Rivka is not from the village, and they meet her at the concentration camp. At the end Ester, Shifre and Rivka get 'chosen', which means that they would be gassed. Hannah swapps places with Rivka to save her and gets gassed, but just before she dies she goes back to the present.<br /> She sees her Aunt Eva has the number J18202 on her arm, the ame number as Rivka. It turns out that Aunt Eva was saved by her friend, Chaya, at a concentration camp. Hanah was named after Chaya.<br /><br /> The reason I read this in the first place was because we learned about concentration camps at school. They sound like something out of a horror movie! I feel so sorry for Jews during WWII.</span>

<div id="review_1452120574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1452120574" />
    <a title="Jamie Barringer (Ravenmount)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5877880-jamie-barringer-ravenmount"><img alt="Jamie Barringer (Ravenmount)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1517797473p2/5877880.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1452120574?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie Barringer (Ravenmount)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie Barringer (Ravenmount)" href="/user/show/5877880-jamie-barringer-ravenmount">Jamie Barringer (Ravenmount)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1452120574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11190907550000697067">This book is a bit like the Chronicles of Narnia, but with a dark twist. A modern, American Jewish girl opens a door and is magically transported back in time into the body of another Jewish girl, just before that girl and her entire village is transported to a Nazi concentration camp. All of a sudden the Holocaust, instead of tiresome stories drummed into her by her older relatives at holiday gatherings, becomes very horrifically real. Having read quite a few nonfiction accounts of the concentr</span>
  <span id="freeText11190907550000697067" style="display:none">This book is a bit like the Chronicles of Narnia, but with a dark twist. A modern, American Jewish girl opens a door and is magically transported back in time into the body of another Jewish girl, just before that girl and her entire village is transported to a Nazi concentration camp. All of a sudden the Holocaust, instead of tiresome stories drummed into her by her older relatives at holiday gatherings, becomes very horrifically real. Having read quite a few nonfiction accounts of the concentration camps and other horrors of the Nazi regime, I found this book very readable, a story that makes the camps and the people in them real, without the story feeling preachy or flat. Yolen does a great job of balancing the horror and hopelessness of the situation Holocaust victims found themselves in, while still maintaining a glimmer of hope throughout, since the story is told from the perspective of a descendant of a survivor who somehow not only was not destroyed by the camps and the Nazis, but who went on to have a family and a good life afterwards.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11190907550000697067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BIEHO9XdRw5qhkDjep7yFf1bksUN5TUCuSd9IKmvMpGVNZlNXsqovkNTiBQM9mKHIshO2IClBX9YiIQvvDl45w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128066.The_Pianist"><img alt="The Pianist: The Extraordinary Story of One Man&#39;s Survival in Warsaw, 1939–45" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1491050715l/128066._SX98_.jpg" />
